# Project Complexity Levels

This document provides the lightweight complexity rubric used by early lifecycle phases. It helps teams scale documentation, review depth, and gate evidence without skipping required lifecycle phases.

For lifecycle applicability and scaling rules, see **`03. Scope.md`** and **`02. Purpose.md`** §**1.4**. Phase documents may request a rough complexity estimate before enough information exists for a full assessment; in that case, use **Unknown** and record what must be learned next.

---

## 1. Quick Complexity Rubric

| Level | Typical indicators | Lifecycle treatment |
| --- | --- | --- |
| **Small** | Simple feature, small website, small automation, limited users, no sensitive data, low integration burden. | Use the standard lifecycle with lightweight artifacts and minimal review depth. |
| **Medium** | Multi-screen app, database-backed system, moderate users, basic integrations, moderate operational or support needs. | Use standard artifacts with clear requirements, design, testing, release evidence, and named owners. |
| **Large** | SaaS platform, regulated or security-sensitive system, multiple roles, complex integrations, high availability needs, significant data handling, or cross-functional delivery. | Use fuller documentation, stronger gate evidence, security/privacy/architecture review, and explicit operational planning. |
| **Unknown** | Insufficient information to classify complexity, unclear users, unclear data, unknown integrations, or uncertain regulatory/operational exposure. | Continue discovery/research before treating the idea as ready for downstream planning. |

---

## 2. Examples and Required Documents by Level

The legacy planning procedure names the three practical documentation levels as **Level 1 — Small Project**, **Level 2 — Standard Project**, and **Level 3 — Critical / Governed Project**. In this document, those map to **Small**, **Medium**, and **Large** respectively.

### 2.1 Level 1 — Small Project

**Typical examples:**

- Small website.
- Internal tool.
- Simple automation.
- One-person MVP.

**Minimum required documents:**

```text
Problem Definition
Scope Document
Feature List
Basic Design
Test Checklist
Deployment Checklist
Post-Release Review
```

Use lightweight versions of these artifacts where risk is low, scope is narrow, users are limited, and no sensitive data, production-critical workflow, regulated process, multi-tenant data, or security-sensitive surface is involved.

### 2.2 Level 2 — Standard Project

**Typical examples:**

- Business web app.
- CRM portal.
- SaaS MVP.
- Mobile app.
- Database-backed internal or customer-facing workflow.

**Minimum required documents:**

```text
Problem Definition
Project Selection Scorecard
Requirements Specification
Architecture Document
UI/UX Design
Data Model
Development Plan
Test Strategy
Release Checklist
Maintenance Plan
```

Use standard lifecycle artifacts with named owners, traceability seeds, design review, testing evidence, release readiness, and maintenance ownership.

### 2.3 Level 3 — Critical / Governed Project

**Typical examples:**

- Compliance system.
- Healthcare app.
- Financial system.
- Security-sensitive platform.
- Multi-tenant SaaS.
- Regulated, high-availability, or customer-data platform.

**Minimum required documents:**

```text
Full lifecycle documentation
Security review
Risk register
Traceability matrix
Compliance matrix
Architecture decision records
Formal release approval
Operational monitoring plan
Incident response plan
```

Use full lifecycle evidence, stronger review depth, explicit risk ownership, security/privacy/compliance validation, operational planning, and formal release approval. Required CYBERCUBE standards evidence is governed by `25. Quality and Compliance Checks.md` §5.

---

## 3. Early Estimate Fields

When a phase asks for a complexity estimate, record:

- **Initial complexity:** Small / Medium / Large / Unknown.
- **Rationale:** 1–3 sentences explaining the estimate.
- **Main drivers:** users, data sensitivity, integrations, availability, compliance, team size, or delivery uncertainty.
- **Unknowns:** information needed to confirm or revise the estimate.

The canonical early estimate field appears in **Template A-0 — Idea Capture Form** in **`28. Appendix A — Template Library.md`**. Later phases may revise the estimate as requirements, architecture, data, integration, security, and operational facts become clearer.

---

## 4. Lifecycle Selection Checkpoints

Complexity is selected progressively. Early phases may use **Unknown** or a provisional level; by the end of Phase 4, the project should have a confirmed level or an explicit owner for resolving remaining uncertainty.

| Lifecycle point | Complexity action | Evidence |
| --- | --- | --- |
| Phase 1 — Idea Capture | Select an initial complexity estimate: Small / Medium / Large / Unknown. | Idea Capture Form, initial rationale, known risks. |
| Phase 2 — Problem Definition | Re-check the estimate against validated users, severity, constraints, and evidence. | Problem Definition Document, evidence/observations, updated rationale if changed. |
| Phase 3 — Project Evaluation and Selection | Use complexity as an evaluation input for scoring, cost/benefit, risk, delivery effort, and review depth. | Project Selection Scorecard or equivalent evaluation record. |
| Phase 4 — Feasibility and Business Case | Confirm the complexity level before G3, or record why it remains Unknown and what must be resolved. | Feasibility Assessment, Business Case, CYBERCUBE standards applicability evidence. |
| Phase 5 — Requirements Definition | Apply the confirmed complexity level to requirements/NFR depth, traceability rigor, review depth, and required documents. | Requirements Specification Package, NFR register, traceability seed. |

After Phase 5, complexity may still be revised if design, implementation, testing, release, or operational evidence reveals higher risk. Any downgrade requires rationale; any upgrade must update affected artifacts and gate evidence.

---

## 5. Scaling Matrix

Complexity changes the depth of evidence, not the lifecycle path.

| Area | Small | Medium | Large |
| --- | --- | --- | --- |
| Requirements | Lightweight but testable CRS/SRS sections; explicit assumptions. | Standard Requirements Specification Package with traceability seed. | Full requirements package with stronger traceability, stakeholder review, and NFR coverage. |
| Architecture | Simple design notes or ARD-001-lite where architecture is straightforward. | ARD-001 plus key ADRs and data/API/UI artifacts where applicable. | Full architecture review, ADR coverage, data/API/UI/module artifacts, and explicit operational design. |
| Security / privacy | Basic secure-coding and data checks; no sensitive data by default. | Security/privacy review where data, auth, integrations, or operational risk exist. | Security, privacy, compliance, tenant/data isolation, and release security evidence expected unless formally waived. |
| Testing | Focused unit/integration/acceptance evidence for changed scope. | Standard Test Strategy, acceptance scenarios, QA results, and traceability. | Full test strategy, broader regression/security/performance/accessibility coverage, and stronger release evidence. |
| Release / deployment | Lightweight release/deployment records if low impact. | Standard release readiness, rollback, deployment, and configuration evidence. | Full release, rollback, security review, deployment, migration, monitoring, and support handoff evidence. |
| Maintenance | Known issues and owner recorded. | Maintenance plan/backlog and metrics review for supported product work. | Full maintenance plan, incident/change records, metrics/actuals review, and post-release improvement loop. |

---

## 6. Criticality Overrides Complexity

Project complexity is not the same as product criticality. A small project can still require high rigor if it touches sensitive data, regulated workflows, authentication/authorization, production infrastructure, customer-facing availability, payments, tenancy, security controls, or legal/compliance commitments.

Use PRCS / product classification guidance when available:

- **Low criticality:** team-level review may be sufficient if no sensitive data, auth, compliance, or production risk exists.
- **Medium+ criticality:** architecture review and named ownership are expected.
- **High+ criticality:** security review, SLA/SLO definition, stronger testing, and release evidence are expected.
- **Regulated+ criticality:** compliance verification, audit trail, privacy/data handling review, and documented approvals are expected.
- **Safety-critical or equivalent risk:** CTO / executive sign-off and external or independent review may be required.

If complexity and criticality disagree, use the higher-rigor treatment. Do not use a **Small** complexity estimate to downgrade required security, privacy, data, authorization, release, or operational controls.

---

## 7. Unknown Resolution Rule

Use **Unknown** when the team lacks enough information to classify the work. Unknown is not a shortcut to lightweight treatment.

Before moving downstream, record:

- what is unknown,
- who owns the discovery action,
- what evidence will resolve it,
- when it must be resolved,
- which gate or phase will re-check the estimate.

Unresolved security, privacy, data classification, tenancy, compliance, integration, availability, migration, or production-impact unknowns should be treated at **Medium** or **Large** review depth until resolved.

---

## 8. Scaling Principle

All projects still follow the Master Lifecycle. Complexity changes the **depth** of evidence, review, and documentation; it does not remove required gates or allow phase skipping unless a documented tailoring decision or waiver applies.

Tailoring decisions and waivers must be explicit, time-bound where applicable, and traceable to the relevant gate evidence.

---

## 9. Related Documents

- **`21. Decision Gates.md`** — gates that define required evidence and decisions.
- **`22. Required Documents.md`** — artifact register for lifecycle evidence.
- **`28. Appendix A — Template Library.md`** — Template A-0 and phase-specific evidence templates.
- **`04. Definitions.md`** — controlled lifecycle terms.
- **`../.cursor/rules/prcs-product-classification.mdc`** — PRCS product classification and criticality guidance.







# Traceability Rules

## Canonical source

End-to-end traceability structures, matrix templates, and change-propagation expectations are defined in **USSM — Unified Software Standards Manual v1.0.md**, especially:

- **§4.5** — CRS traceability references  
- **§5.5** — SRS traceability matrix  
- **§6.6** — Design traceability  
- **§7.4.2** — Test case traceability  
- **§10.1 Annex A** — Consolidated traceability matrix templates (CRS ↔ SRS ↔ design ↔ code ↔ tests ↔ changes)

## Minimum expectations

1. **Bidirectional links** between discovery evidence, requirements, scope, design, implementation, tests, release, deployment, maintenance, and post-release improvement records.
2. **Stable identifiers** used consistently across documents, task systems, code references, tests, release records, incidents, and improvement backlogs.
3. **Gate evidence continuity:** every gate should be able to trace its decision to the artifacts listed in `21. Decision Gates.md` and `22. Required Documents.md`.
4. **Change impact:** updating a baselined artifact triggers assessment across linked rows (per USSM governance §3 and Annex A §10.1.5).

Tailor column sets per project while preserving upstream ↔ downstream linkage described in USSM.

---

## Lifecycle Traceability Model

Traceability should show how an idea becomes released and then improved:

```text
Idea / Problem
  -> Selection / Business Case
  -> Requirements / Scope / Features
  -> Architecture / Data / API / UI / Module Design
  -> Development Plan / Tasks / Implementation
  -> Tests / Defects / Validation
  -> Release Approval
  -> Deployment / Configuration / Migration / Rollback
  -> Maintenance / Incidents / Metrics / Backlog
  -> Post-Release Review / Improvements / Next Cycle
```

The minimum link target is the nearest meaningful upstream and downstream artifact. For example, an implementation record may link to a task, feature, requirement, design artifact, test, and defect, without repeating the entire lifecycle chain in the same row.

---

## Suggested Stable ID Vocabulary

Use existing project conventions where they already exist. Otherwise, these prefixes are recommended as **lifecycle-local artifact IDs**. They are not automatically CYBERCUBE Namespace G registry IDs unless a governance registry assigns them.

| Area | Suggested IDs |
| --- | --- |
| Discovery / validation | `IDEA-`, `PROB-`, `VAL-` |
| Selection / business case | `SEL-`, `FEAS-`, `BC-`, `FORECAST-` |
| Requirements / scope | `CRS-`, `SRS-FR-`, `SRS-NFR-`, `REQ-`, `SCOPE-`, `FEAT-` |
| Design / architecture | `ADR-`, `DES-`, `DATA-`, `API-`, `UX-`, `MOD-` |
| Planning / implementation | `TASK-`, `PT-`, `MILE-`, `IMPL-`, `PR-` |
| Testing / validation | `TC-`, `ATS-`, `QA-`, `BUG-`, `VALSIGN-` |
| Release / deployment | `REL-`, `RR-`, `ROLLBACK-`, `DEP-`, `MIG-`, `ENV-` |
| Maintenance / operations | `MNT-`, `INC-`, `MCR-`, `METRIC-`, `PATCH-` |
| Post-release / improvement | `PRR-`, `LESSON-`, `REC-`, `PI-` |
| Governance / decisions | `G1`–`G10`, `WAIVER-`, `RISK-`, `CR-` |

IDs should be stable after approval. If an ID changes, record the migration from old ID to new ID.

### CYBERCUBE Naming Namespace Boundaries

| Namespace | Use in lifecycle work | Rule |
| --- | --- | --- |
| Namespace A | File, branch, package, release artifact, and deliverable names | Follow the artifact-naming convention when producing controlled files or packages. |
| Namespace B | Public entity IDs in APIs, URLs, logs, support workflows, or UI | Use CC-PID; do not expose raw database primary keys externally. CC-PID is not authentication or authorization evidence. |
| Namespace G | Registered governance IDs such as `PRD-XXX`, `MOD-XXX`, and future registry-controlled IDs | Use only when assigned by the authoritative registry; do not invent sequence numbers in local lifecycle artifacts. |
| Namespace M | Source modules, components, directories, exports, and implementation files | Confirm naming in Phase 8 before Phase 9 coding and preserve it through release evidence. |

Use `../CYBERCUBE standards/[1]-CYBERCUBE-Name-Registry.md` as the authoritative lookup for registered Namespace A/B/G/M values, UUID-to-CC-PID mappings, and project registry entries.

When a lifecycle-local ID needs to be promoted into a CYBERCUBE governance registry, record the mapping from local ID to Namespace G ID and retain both references in traceability records.

---

## Trace Links by Phase and Gate

| Phase / Gate | Traceability expectation |
| --- | --- |
| Phase 1 / G1 | Idea Capture Form (Template A-0) links idea, sponsor, problem hypothesis, complexity estimate, and decision. |
| Phase 2 / G2 | Problem Definition Document (Template A-0.1) links problem, impacted users, validation sources, and G2 decision. |
| Phases 3–4 / G3 | Selection scorecard, feasibility, business case, and forecasts link candidate idea/problem to funding decision and assumptions. |
| Phase 5 / G4 | CRS/SRS/NFRs, acceptance criteria, and traceability seed link stakeholder needs to testable requirements. |
| Phase 6 | Scope, feature inventory, tasks, and delivery plans link approved requirements to planned work. |
| Phase 7 / G5 | Architecture, ADRs, data model, API contract, UI/UX, and module design link requirements/features to design decisions. |
| Phase 8 / G6 | Module/file plan, environment strategy, development plan, TD-001/HG-001, and blueprint artifacts link design to executable work. |
| Phase 9 | Implementation records, code reviews, commits/PRs, and known defects link tasks/design to code and initial tests. |
| Phase 10 / G7 | Test strategy, acceptance scenarios, QA results, bug reports, and validation sign-off link requirements/features/code to verification evidence. |
| Phase 11 / G8 | Release readiness, known issues, release notes, rollback plan, security review, and final approval link validation evidence to release approval. |
| Phase 12 / G9 | Deployment checklist, deployment record, migration record, environment configuration, and rollback confirmation link release approval to deployed state. |
| Phase 13 | Maintenance plan, incidents, maintenance changes, metrics, backlog, and patch records link operational reality to support and improvement decisions. |
| Phase 14 / G10 | Post-release review, lessons learned, recommendations, maintenance backlog, and process notes link outcomes to the next planning cycle. |

---

## Artifact Link Expectations

| Artifact type | Should link to |
| --- | --- |
| Gate decision | Required evidence, approver, decision outcome, follow-up action. |
| Requirement | Source need/problem, acceptance criteria, design, tests, implementation, defects. |
| Feature | Requirements, scope boundary, design, tasks, tests, release notes. |
| ADR / design artifact | Requirements/features, constraints, alternatives, affected modules/APIs/data/UI. |
| Task / implementation record | Requirement/feature, design artifact, source file(s), test(s), PR/review, defects. |
| Test / QA result | Requirement/feature, test case/scenario, build, defect, validation sign-off. |
| Defect / incident | Requirement/feature if applicable, release/deployment, affected component, fix, verification. |
| Release / deployment record | G8 approval, release notes, rollback plan, artifact/build, environment, validation, incident/rollback status. |
| Maintenance backlog item | Incident/metric/customer feedback/change request, owner, priority, follow-up release or recommendation. |
| Improvement recommendation | Lesson/metric/incident/backlog item, owner, next-cycle routing, process/change-control link. |

---

## Machine-Readable Evidence and Requirements ↔ Tasks

**Classification:** Keep — machine-readable traceability guidance for requirements, tasks, standards evidence, gates, and verification artifacts.

When programs use **TD-001** (`Agnostic Execution Decomposition — Create Tasks List Procedure.md`) with **`tasks_board.json`**, you may add **direct requirement links** on each task so automation can validate coverage without relying only on spreadsheets.

### Goals

1. **Traceability:** From any requirement ID, list linked tasks; from any task ID, list covered requirements.
2. **Coverage:** Flag requirements with **no** linked tasks (gaps) and tasks with **no** requirement (**orphans**), unless explicitly tagged not applicable with a recorded reason.
3. **Stability:** Immutable string IDs for requirements and tasks; document any rename or migration if IDs change.
4. **Automation:** A **report** command (script or CI step) that validates consistency and prints a summary—no silent drift.

### Deliverables (adapt paths to the repo)

| Artifact | Role |
| --- | --- |
| **Requirements catalog** | Single source of truth: at minimum `id`, `title`, `status` (e.g. draft/approved/deprecated), optional `priority`, `source`, `notes`. Prefer one structured file (JSON, YAML, or CSV) under version control. |
| **Tasks catalog** | Tasks with `id`, `title`, `status`, optional `iteration` / `phase`, and **`linked_requirement_ids`** (array of requirement IDs). |
| **Linking rules** | Documented cardinality (e.g. each task ≥1 requirement unless `requirements_not_applicable: true` with reason); how epics/stories vs technical chores are handled. |
| **Traceability report** | Read-only checker: load requirements + tasks; validate ID formats and references (no dangling IDs); list orphans and uncovered requirements; exit non-zero on violation **if** CI gating is chosen—otherwise exit 0 with warnings (pick one policy and document it). |
| **Standards applicability record** | Appendix D JSON/YAML copy where automation is used; links applicable CYBERCUBE standards to gates, owners, and evidence artifacts. |
| **Identifier conformance notes** | Evidence that lifecycle-local IDs, Namespace G IDs, CC-PIDs, and Namespace M names are not mixed and that public entity IDs do not expose raw database keys. |
| **Contributor docs** | Short README or CONTRIBUTING section: how to add a requirement, add a task, link them, run the report locally and in CI. |

### Constraints

- Do **not** invent requirements from tasks without explicit approval text in-repo or from stakeholders.
- Prefer **plain** formats and scripts runnable without mandatory cloud services.
- Keep **version one minimal**; optional fields and diagrams can follow.
- **Match** repository conventions (package manager, `scripts/`, naming).

### Acceptance criteria (procedure-level)

- Requirements and tasks live in **version-controlled** artifacts with **stable IDs**.
- Every **new task template** shows where **`linked_requirement_ids`** belong.
- Running the traceability **report** completes locally with clear output.
- Docs explain linking rules and how to resolve orphans and gaps.

### Phase mapping

- **Phase 6 — Planning and Scope Control:** baseline requirements catalog and task linking policy alongside `tasks_list.md` / `tasks_board.json`.
- **Phase 10 — Testing and Validation:** tie verification evidence (test results, reports) to the same requirement IDs where tasks already link—consistent with USSM §7.4 and Annex A matrices.
- **Phase 11 / G8 and Phase 14 / G10:** standards applicability evidence can be checked against Appendix D's machine-readable record where automation is used.

---

## Related Documents

- **`21. Decision Gates.md`** — canonical G1–G10 gate evidence.
- **`22. Required Documents.md`** — lifecycle artifact register.
- **`28. Appendix A — Template Library.md`** — canonical templates that define trace fields and approval records.
- **`31. Appendix D — CYBERCUBE Standards Applicability Matrix.md`** — standards applicability evidence and optional machine-readable schema.
- **`../CYBERCUBE standards/[1]-CYBERCUBE-Name-Registry.md`** — authoritative lookup for CYBERCUBE namespace values and project registry entries.
- **`23. Project Complexity Levels.md`** — scaling depth; complexity does not remove traceability.
- **`26. Change Control.md`** — change impact routing for baselined artifacts.







# Quality and Compliance Checks

## 1. Purpose

This document maps quality and compliance checks across the Master Lifecycle. It does not replace phase documents, decision gates, required artifacts, or detailed standards; it identifies what must be checked before a project advances, releases, deploys, or closes the improvement loop.

Use this file with:

- **`21. Decision Gates.md`** — gate evidence and decisions.
- **`22. Required Documents.md`** — lifecycle artifact register.
- **`23. Project Complexity Levels.md`** — scaling depth and criticality overrides.
- **`24. Traceability Rules.md`** — end-to-end traceability expectations.
- **`28. Appendix A — Template Library.md`** — evidence templates.

---

## 2. Canonical Checklists

Formal review checklists for requirements, design, and testing are specified in **USSM Section 10.5 — Annex E — Compliance Checklists** inside `USSM — Unified Software Standards Manual v1.0.md`:

- **§10.5.1** — SRS (IEEE 29148 alignment)
- **§10.5.2** — Design / DDS (IEEE 1016 alignment)
- **§10.5.3** — Testing (IEEE 29119 alignment)

Use these as **default review gates** unless a project’s regulatory context mandates additional checks.

---

## 3. Supplementary UI/UX Audit

For **visual, interaction, and experiential** acceptance criteria beyond Annex E’s document-centric checks, use **`29. Appendix B — Checklists.md`** during Phase 7 design review and Phase 10 validation. Map findings to defects or requirement updates under normal change control.

---

## 4. Lifecycle Quality Map

| Phase / Gate | Minimum quality and compliance checks | Evidence source |
| --- | --- | --- |
| Phase 1 / G1 | Idea is clear enough for analysis; sponsor/context recorded; complexity estimate present; obvious security/privacy/compliance unknowns captured. | Idea Capture Form (Template A-0). |
| Phase 2 / G2 | Problem is validated by independent evidence; impacted users and consequences are clear; problem/solution confusion is corrected. | Problem Definition Document (Template A-0.1). |
| Phases 3–4 / G3 | Selection rationale, feasibility, business case, assumptions, risks, and funding decision are documented; high-risk unknowns have owners. | Templates A-3.1, A-3.2, A-3.3, A-4, A-5 where used. |
| Phase 5 / G4 | Requirements are testable, traceable, scoped, and reviewed; NFRs, acceptance criteria, and stakeholder/user needs are covered. | Requirements package (A-8), CRS (A-1), SRS (A-2), NFR (A-10 / NFR-001), USSM Annex E §10.5.1. |
| Phase 6 | Scope is controlled; features map to requirements; delivery plan has owners, milestones, dependencies, and traceability hooks. | Scope Document (A-6), Feature Inventory (A-9), TD-001/HG-001 where used. |
| Phase 7 / G5 | Architecture satisfies requirements and NFRs; ADRs capture material decisions; data/API/UI/module artifacts exist where applicable; accessibility and UX checks are planned. | ARD-001, ADRs, A-11, A-12, UXD-001, MDM-001, USSM Annex E §10.5.2, Appendix B. |
| Phase 8 / G6 | Environment, branching, module/file plan, development plan, setup guidance, coding standards, and initial test setup are ready. | Templates A-13, A-14, A-15, Phase 8 DGP-001 guidance, TD-001/HG-001 where used. |
| Phase 9 | Code follows approved design, secure coding, traceability, review, and Definition of Done; defects/debt are visible. | Implementation records, code review evidence, tests, traceability updates, known defects/debt. |
| Phase 10 / G7 | Test strategy executed; acceptance scenarios pass or exceptions are recorded; defects have severity/disposition; validation sign-off is recorded. | Templates A-16 through A-20, USSM Annex E §10.5.3, Appendix B where UI/UX applies. |
| Phase 11 / G8 | Release readiness, known issues, release notes, rollback plan, security review, and final approval are complete. | Templates A-21 through A-26 and A-25.1 where security review applies. |
| Phase 12 / G9 | Deployment is executed from approved release evidence; migration/configuration/rollback status and PDV are recorded. | Templates A-27 through A-31, USSM §8. |
| Phase 13 | Maintenance ownership, incidents, changes, backlog, metrics/actuals, and patch records are maintained and reviewed. | Templates A-32 through A-37, REF-001 where refactoring applies. |
| Phase 14 / G10 | Post-release review closes the loop; lessons, recommendations, backlog, and process improvements are routed. | Templates A-35 and A-38 through A-41, `27. Process Improvement.md`. |

---

## 5. CYBERCUBE Standards Applicability Matrix

Every project must determine which CYBERCUBE standards apply based on product type, data handled, integrations, security surface, criticality, deployment model, vendors, and AI usage. Applicability must be reviewed early, converted into requirements and design controls where applicable, and confirmed before release approval.

Use **`31. Appendix D — CYBERCUBE Standards Applicability Matrix.md`** as the reusable project-level evidence form and optional machine-readable record.

If a standard is marked **Conditional** or **No**, the project must record a brief non-applicability rationale. If applicability changes later, update the matrix and route any scope, requirement, design, testing, or release impact through normal change control.

| Standard / Policy | Trigger | Applies? | Phases affected | Evidence | Gate check | Owner |
| --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- |
| PRCS Product Classification | Candidate production product, existing product extension, architectural facet change, or pre-product/cross-cutting work needing Work Type Tags | Yes | 1, 3–4, 11 | Candidate PRCS metadata at G3; approved `PRD-XXX` + `PCL-L.D.E.C` before production release at G8; Work Type Tag rationale where no PRD applies | G3 / G8 | Product Owner |
| Naming & Identifier Standard (`STD-ENG-001`) | Any project producing artifacts, public IDs, governance IDs, modules, components, files, APIs, logs, or release evidence | Yes | 5–11 | Stable artifact IDs; Namespace G governance IDs where applicable; CC-PID use for public entity IDs; no raw database IDs exposed; Namespace M module/component/file naming evidence | G6 / G8 / G10 | Engineering Lead |
| Architecture Governance | Material architecture, technology, or integration decision | Conditional | 4, 7, 11 | ADRs, architecture review, deviations | G3 / G5 / G8 | Architecture Lead |
| ERM / Risk | Material business, technology, security, privacy, vendor, or AI risk | Yes | 3–4, 6, 11, 14 | Risk register entries, treatment decisions, acceptance records | G3 / G8 / G10 | Risk Owner |
| Security Policy | Any software system | Yes | 5, 7, 9, 11 | Security requirements, review evidence | G4 / G5 / G8 | Security Reviewer |
| Secure Coding | Any implementation work | Yes | 8–10 | Coding standards, review/test evidence | G6 / G7 | Engineering Lead |
| Auth / Identity | User, admin, service identity, sessions, login, tokens, SSO, API keys | Conditional | 5, 7, 10, 11 | Auth requirements/design/tests, audit notes | G4 / G5 / G7 / G8 | Security / Identity Owner |
| AuthZ / Access Control | Roles, permissions, tenant/user ownership, privileged operations | Conditional | 5, 7, 10, 11 | Access-control model, policy tests, audit evidence | G4 / G5 / G7 / G8 | Security / Architect |
| Crypto / KMS | Secrets, encryption, signing, TLS, key lifecycle, sensitive data | Conditional | 5, 7, 8, 11 | Crypto inventory, key/secret handling notes, review evidence | G4 / G5 / G6 / G8 | Security / DevOps |
| Vulnerability Management | Any shipped software, dependencies, containers, infra, or third-party code | Yes | 8–11, 13 | SCA/SAST/DAST results, remediation/acceptance records | G6 / G7 / G8 / G10 | Security / Engineering Lead |
| Privacy / Privacy Handling | Personal data, tracking, consent, DSAR, cross-border processing | Conditional | 4, 5, 7, 11, 14 | Privacy requirements, DPIA/assessment, consent/DSAR notes | G3 / G4 / G5 / G8 / G10 | Privacy / Legal |
| Data Classification | Any stored/transmitted data | Conditional | 5, 7, 11 | Data classification notes | G4 / G5 / G8 | Data Owner |
| Data Isolation | Multi-tenant/customer data | Conditional | 5, 7, 10, 11 | Tenant isolation design/tests | G5 / G7 / G8 | Architect |
| Records / Retention | Business records, audit evidence, customer/employee/financial data | Conditional | 5, 11, 13, 14 | Retention notes, disposal/hold considerations, evidence retention | G4 / G8 / G10 | Records / Legal |
| Observability / Telemetry | Service, API, job, integration, production operation, tenant-scoped flow | Conditional | 7, 8, 11, 13 | Logging/metrics/tracing plan, dashboard/alert evidence | G5 / G6 / G8 / G10 | SRE / Engineering Lead |
| Testing / Quality | Any implementation, release, migration, or critical workflow | Yes | 8–11 | Test strategy, CI evidence, coverage/defect disposition | G6 / G7 / G8 | QA Lead |
| Release / Deployment | Any deployment, release, patch, migration, or rollback-capable change | Conditional | 11–12, 13 | Release plan, deployment/rollback evidence, approval record | G8 / G9 / G10 | Release Manager |
| Change Management | Baseline, scope, requirement, design, release, or operational-impacting change | Conditional | 5–14 | Change request, impact/risk analysis, approval/rollback notes | G4–G10 | Change Owner |
| Vendor Risk | External vendor, SaaS, API provider, subcontractor, processor, or critical dependency | Conditional | 4, 5, 7, 11, 13 | Vendor assessment, contract/DPA/security evidence | G3 / G4 / G5 / G8 / G10 | Vendor Owner / Legal |
| AI Usage | AI-assisted or AI feature work | Conditional | 4, 5, 7, 10, 11 | AI risk/use review | G3–G8 | AI/Legal Reviewer |
| AI Engineering | AI product feature, model workflow, RAG, automated decision, evaluation pipeline | Conditional | 4, 5, 7, 10, 11, 14 | AI design review, evaluation plan/results, monitoring notes | G3 / G4 / G5 / G7 / G8 / G10 | AI Engineering Owner |
| Clean Room Design Process | Source-reader separation, IP-sensitive rebuild, vendor handoff, litigation-safe analysis | Conditional | 7–8, 11 | BP-001-DRCR selection, separation log, sanitized handoff evidence | G5 / G6 / G8 | Architecture / Legal |

### Applicability Reviewed Checkpoints

| Lifecycle point | Required check |
| --- | --- |
| Phase 4 | Identify high-risk applicable standards during feasibility and business case review. |
| Phase 5 | Convert applicable standards into requirements, NFRs, constraints, and acceptance criteria. |
| Phase 7 | Reflect applicable standards in architecture, security, data, API, integration, and UI design where relevant. |
| Phase 8 | Prepare tooling, environment, coding, testing, CI, and review controls needed to satisfy applicable standards. |
| Phase 11 | Confirm required CYBERCUBE standards evidence is complete before release approval. |
| Phase 14 | Review applicability gaps, evidence gaps, exceptions, and improvement actions for the next cycle. |

The project-level matrix is complete when Appendix D completion criteria are satisfied and the record is reviewed before G8 and again during G10.

---

## 6. Cross-Cutting Checks

Apply these checks at every phase where relevant:

| Check | Expectation |
| --- | --- |
| Traceability | Evidence links upstream and downstream per `24. Traceability Rules.md`; gate decisions cite required artifacts. |
| Security / privacy | Sensitive data, auth/authz, secrets, tenancy, security review, privacy, and compliance impacts are assessed at the appropriate depth. |
| Accessibility / UX | User-facing work applies Appendix B and UXD-001 where UI/UX is in scope. |
| Risk / waiver handling | Material risks, exceptions, deviations, and waivers are explicit, approved, time-bound where applicable, and routed through the correct governance path. |
| Change control | Scope-affecting or release-impacting changes after baseline flow through `26. Change Control.md`. |
| Evidence completeness | Required artifacts have owner, date/version, decision, approval, and follow-up actions where applicable. |
| Complexity scaling | Review depth follows `23. Project Complexity Levels.md`; criticality can increase rigor even when complexity is small. |
| CYBERCUBE standards applicability | Applicable standards are identified, evidenced, or explicitly marked not applicable with rationale per §5. |

---

## 7. Relationship to Phase Gates

Checklist completion supports, but does not replace, gate decisions. Each gate must still answer:

1. Can the project move forward?
2. What evidence proves it?
3. Who approves it?
4. What happens if it fails?

Use **`21. Decision Gates.md`** as the authority for gate outcomes and required evidence.







# Change Control

## 1. Purpose

Change Control governs changes to baselined lifecycle artifacts, approved scope, release posture, production environments, maintenance commitments, and process outputs. It ensures changes are assessed, approved, documented, reversible where feasible, and traceable to affected requirements, designs, tests, releases, deployments, maintenance records, and improvement actions.

This procedure applies across the Master Lifecycle and aligns with CYBERCUBE Change Management Policy **5.7**.

## 2. When Change Control Applies

Use change control when a proposed change affects:

- approved scope, requirements, acceptance criteria, or feature inventory,
- architecture, data model, API/integration contract, UI/UX design, or module boundaries,
- release approval evidence, deployment procedure, rollback path, or production configuration,
- security, privacy, compliance, tenant/data isolation, or operational commitments,
- maintenance backlog priority, incident remediation, patch/release content, or post-release improvement routing,
- any gate decision or required evidence in **`21. Decision Gates.md`**.

Development work before baseline approval may use normal backlog refinement unless the phase document or project governance requires a formal change record.

## 3. Change Types

| Type | Use | Approval path |
| --- | --- | --- |
| **Standard change** | Low-risk, repeatable, pre-approved change with a known procedure. | Pre-approved catalog or team lead; log after execution. |
| **Normal change** | Planned change with material scope, technical, release, operational, or compliance impact. | Change owner submits request; approval by Engineering Lead, Product Owner, CAB, or sponsor depending on risk. |
| **Emergency change** | Urgent change needed for incident response, security threat, imminent outage, compliance deadline, or production failure. | Emergency approval by incident/change authority; formal record completed within 24 hours. |

Emergency change control is not a shortcut for poor planning. Repeated emergency changes for the same cause require root cause analysis and process improvement.

## 4. Risk Classification

| Risk | Typical indicators | Minimum treatment |
| --- | --- | --- |
| **Low** | Small impact, easy rollback, no sensitive data, no customer-visible disruption. | Basic assessment, documented test/verification, owner approval. |
| **Medium** | Some user/customer impact, configuration or dependency changes, moderate rollback or support needs. | Impact/risk assessment, testing evidence, rollback plan, named approver. |
| **High** | Many users/customers, production release impact, data migration, security/privacy impact, difficult rollback. | Full assessment, staging/validation evidence, rollback tested where feasible, CAB or equivalent review. |
| **Critical** | Core system, data loss risk, regulated impact, security incident, broad outage, irreversible change. | Executive/CAB approval, full evidence, incident/rollback readiness, post-implementation review. |

Complexity does not reduce risk treatment. Use **`23. Project Complexity Levels.md`** for scaling depth, and apply the higher rigor when product criticality, data sensitivity, or operational exposure demands it.

## 5. Change Request Record

Use a stable ID for each formal change request:

```text
CR-YYYY-NNNNN
```

The change request should include:

| Section | Required content |
| --- | --- |
| Change information | ID, title, requester, owner, date, affected product/release/environment. |
| Classification | Standard / Normal / Emergency; Low / Medium / High / Critical risk. |
| Description and rationale | What changes, why it is needed, and expected outcome. |
| Scope and affected artifacts | Requirements, features, design, tasks, code, tests, releases, deployments, maintenance records, or processes affected. |
| Impact assessment | User/customer impact, data/security/privacy/compliance impact, operational impact, dependencies, and timing. |
| Implementation plan | Pre-change, during-change, and post-change steps. |
| Test / validation plan | Evidence needed before and after implementation. |
| Rollback / mitigation plan | Trigger conditions, steps, owner, expected duration, data recovery notes. |
| Communications | Internal, customer, support, or operational communications. |
| Approval record | Approver, role/authority, decision, date, conditions. |
| Post-change review | Outcome, incidents, follow-ups, lessons, and process improvements. |

## 6. Lifecycle Gate Triggers

| Gate / phase | Change-control trigger |
| --- | --- |
| **G3 — Project Selected** | Funding, feasibility, project selection, or business-case assumptions materially change. |
| **G4 — Requirements Approved** | Baselined requirements, NFRs, acceptance criteria, or scope change. |
| **G5 — Architecture Approved** | Architecture, ADRs, data model, API/integration, UI/UX, or module boundaries change materially. |
| **G6 — Development Ready** | Environment strategy, module/file plan, development plan, branching, or initial test setup changes materially. |
| **G7 — Testing Passed** | Defects, waivers, validation scope, or acceptance evidence changes the test decision. |
| **G8 — Release Approved** | Known issues, release notes, rollback plan, security review, or final approval conditions change. |
| **G9 — Deployment Completed** | Deployment, migration, configuration, rollback, or operational handoff requires remediation or re-approval. |
| **G10 — Maintenance Review Completed** | Maintenance backlog, improvement recommendations, or process changes affect the next planning cycle or governance process. |

G1/G2 discovery changes are usually handled by revising the idea/problem records unless they affect downstream baselines.

## 7. Approval Rules

- The requester should not be the sole approver for their own material change.
- Security, privacy, compliance, infrastructure, or customer-impacting changes require the relevant reviewer.
- High/Critical changes require named approval and documented risk acceptance where residual risk remains.
- Emergency approval may be verbal or lightweight during response, but must be documented within 24 hours.
- Rejected or deferred changes must record rationale and re-review conditions.

## 8. Traceability and Evidence

Every approved change should link to affected lifecycle evidence. Use **`24. Traceability Rules.md`** and include links to:

- source requirement, feature, problem, defect, incident, or improvement recommendation,
- affected templates or artifacts in **`22. Required Documents.md`**,
- affected gate decision in **`21. Decision Gates.md`**,
- implementation records, tests, releases, deployments, or maintenance records,
- risk, waiver, or exception records where applicable.

Traceability must be updated after the change is implemented.

## 9. Implementation, Verification, and Rollback

Before implementation:

- confirm approval and change window,
- confirm test/validation plan,
- confirm rollback/mitigation path,
- confirm communications and support impact,
- confirm access and environment readiness.

After implementation:

- verify the intended outcome,
- record test/validation results,
- update affected artifacts and traceability links,
- record rollback confirmation or non-invocation where deployment is involved,
- open incident response if the change causes customer-impacting failure.

## 10. Post-Implementation Review

Post-implementation review is required for:

- failed changes,
- emergency changes,
- High/Critical changes,
- changes that caused incidents, customer impact, security findings, rollback, or material delay,
- repeated changes for the same root cause.

Review outcomes should feed **`27. Process Improvement.md`**, the Maintenance Backlog (Template A-35), Lessons Learned Record (Template A-39), Improvement Recommendations (Template A-40), or Process Improvement Notes (Template A-41) as applicable.

## 11. Quality Checks

- Change request has a stable `CR-` ID.
- Affected lifecycle artifacts and gates are identified.
- Risk level and change type are recorded.
- Testing and rollback/mitigation are documented.
- Required reviewers approved the change.
- Traceability links are updated after implementation.
- Failed/emergency/material changes have post-implementation review.

## 12. Related Documents

- **`21. Decision Gates.md`** — gate evidence and change-trigger points.
- **`22. Required Documents.md`** — affected lifecycle artifacts.
- **`23. Project Complexity Levels.md`** — scaling and criticality override rules.
- **`24. Traceability Rules.md`** — trace links and ID vocabulary.
- **`25. Quality and Compliance Checks.md`** — lifecycle quality map.
- **`27. Process Improvement.md`** — routing improvement actions.
- **`28. Appendix A — Template Library.md`** — templates for affected lifecycle evidence.
- CYBERCUBE Change Management Policy **5.7** — production change management authority and CAB/eCAB expectations.






# Process Improvement

## 1. Purpose

Process Improvement defines how lessons, findings, recommendations, failed-change reviews, incidents, audit gaps, and post-release observations are routed into durable improvements to the lifecycle, standards, templates, tooling, training, and delivery practices.

This procedure closes the loop after **G10 — Maintenance Review Completed** and also supports improvement actions discovered during any earlier phase.

## 2. When to Use This Procedure

Use Process Improvement when a finding affects how CYBERCUBE plans, builds, tests, releases, deploys, maintains, or governs software.

Typical triggers:

- G10 follow-up actions from **`20. Phase 14 — Post-Release Review.md`**.
- Lessons Learned Record (Template A-39).
- Improvement Recommendations (Template A-40).
- Process Improvement Notes (Template A-41).
- Failed or emergency changes from **`26. Change Control.md`**.
- Incident / Production Issue Records (Template A-33).
- Operational Metrics / Actuals Review (Template A-36).
- Audit findings, compliance gaps, repeated defects, recurring support issues, or repeated gate failures.

## 3. Improvement Types

| Type | Examples | Typical owner |
| --- | --- | --- |
| **Lifecycle process** | Phase sequencing, gate criteria, handoff rules, review cadence. | Process owner / governance reviewer |
| **Template / documentation** | Appendix A templates, required-document entries, phase procedures. | Documentation owner / phase owner |
| **Engineering practice** | Coding standards, testing approach, review rules, CI/CD gates. | Engineering lead |
| **Operations / SRE** | Monitoring, alerting, rollback, deployment, incident handling. | Operations / SRE lead |
| **Security / privacy / compliance** | Security review gaps, data handling, audit evidence, waiver process. | Security / compliance owner |
| **Training / enablement** | Onboarding, reviewer guidance, team checklists, examples. | Team lead / enablement owner |

## 4. Improvement Intake

Each improvement candidate should record:

```text
Improvement ID:
Source:
Source Artifact Link:
Description:
Problem / Opportunity:
Affected Phase(s):
Affected Document(s) / Template(s):
Owner:
Priority:
Target Date:
```

Recommended ID format:

```text
PI-YYYY-NNN
```

Use Template A-41 when the improvement affects lifecycle process, standards, tooling, handoffs, or governance. Use Template A-40 when the improvement is primarily a product, operational, support, or backlog recommendation.

## 5. Triage and Prioritization

Prioritize improvements by:

- risk reduction,
- recurrence prevention,
- customer or user impact,
- compliance/audit impact,
- engineering efficiency,
- operational reliability,
- template or process reuse,
- effort and adoption cost.

| Priority | Treatment |
| --- | --- |
| **P0** | Immediate action; may block release, deployment, or next-cycle planning. |
| **P1** | Schedule in the next active planning or governance cycle. |
| **P2** | Track in the improvement backlog with an owner and target review date. |
| **P3** | Keep as observation or optional enhancement. |

## 6. Routing Rules

| Improvement affects | Route to |
| --- | --- |
| Product scope, requirements, feature priority | Product backlog / planning and `26. Change Control.md` when baselined scope changes. |
| Lifecycle phase, gate, template, or required document | Process owner and update to relevant Master Lifecycle file(s). |
| Architecture or technology decision | Architecture governance / ADR update. |
| Security, privacy, compliance, or risk posture | Security/compliance owner and risk register where required. |
| Release, deployment, rollback, or operational readiness | Release/operations owner and change control where release posture changes. |
| Test strategy, CI/CD, quality gate, or coverage rule | QA/engineering owner and quality/compliance review. |
| Training or team behavior | Team lead / enablement owner. |

## 7. Approval Rules

- Minor wording or clarification improvements may be approved by the relevant document owner.
- Changes to gate evidence, mandatory artifacts, lifecycle sequencing, security/privacy controls, or release/deployment requirements require the relevant owner and governance reviewer.
- Improvements that change approved scope, release posture, operational commitments, or production behavior must flow through **`26. Change Control.md`**.
- Permanent exceptions are not process improvements; they require an approved waiver or standards change.

## 8. Implementation and Verification

For each approved process improvement:

1. Update the affected document, template, checklist, script, or workflow.
2. Record before/after summary and rationale.
3. Update related references, especially `21. Decision Gates.md`, `22. Required Documents.md`, and `28. Appendix A — Template Library.md` where applicable.
4. Communicate the change to affected owners or teams.
5. Verify the change resolved the original finding.
6. Record any follow-up action or adoption review date.

## 9. Improvement Backlog

Maintain improvement candidates in an improvement backlog or equivalent tracker. Each item should include:

- stable ID,
- source artifact,
- owner,
- priority,
- status,
- due date or review date,
- affected lifecycle area,
- decision or approval,
- closure evidence.

Suggested statuses:

```text
New -> Triaged -> Approved -> In Progress -> Implemented -> Verified -> Closed
```

Rejected or deferred items must keep rationale and re-review conditions.

## 10. Quality Checks

- Improvement has a stable ID and source evidence.
- Affected phase, gate, document, template, or owner is identified.
- Priority and routing are clear.
- Required approvals are recorded.
- Related documents are updated consistently.
- Change control is used when the improvement changes approved scope, release posture, operational commitments, or production behavior.
- Closure includes verification that the original issue or opportunity was addressed.

## 11. Related Documents

- **`20. Phase 14 — Post-Release Review.md`** — G10 review and improvement routing.
- **`21. Decision Gates.md`** — gate evidence and follow-up routing.
- **`22. Required Documents.md`** — lifecycle artifact register.
- **`24. Traceability Rules.md`** — improvement and recommendation traceability.
- **`25. Quality and Compliance Checks.md`** — quality/compliance findings that may trigger improvements.
- **`26. Change Control.md`** — changes to baselined artifacts, scope, release posture, or operational commitments.
- **`28. Appendix A — Template Library.md`** — Templates A-39, A-40, and A-41.
- **`USSM — Unified Software Standards Manual v1.0.md`** — §10 continuous improvement and Annex G.







# Appendix A — Template Library

## Purpose and Template Index

The **Templates Index** for CRS, SRS, DDS, test plans, deployment plans, maintenance logs, traceability matrices, change requests, compliance checklists, glossary pages, and metadata shells is specified in **USSM Section 10.6 — Annex F** (`USSM — Unified Software Standards Manual v1.0.md`).

Centralize controlled templates in a single repository, version them with the rest of the procedure, and update them when USSM or governing standards change.

This appendix is the Master Lifecycle's reusable evidence library. It contains templates for discovery through post-release review: **A-0 through A-41**, plus **A-25.1**. Some entries are full templates; others are pointers to standalone controlled procedures where the canonical template already exists.

## Local Template Catalog

| Lifecycle area | Templates |
| --- | --- |
| Discovery and problem framing | A-0 Idea Capture Form; A-0.1 Problem Definition Document |
| Requirements and design backbone | A-1 CRS; A-2 SRS; A-3 DDS / SDD |
| Selection, feasibility, and business case | A-3.1 Project Selection Scorecard; A-3.2 Feasibility Assessment; A-3.3 Business Case; A-4 Business Field Report; A-5 Entrepreneurial forecasting guide |
| Scope and requirements support | A-6 Scope Document; A-7 Stakeholder and User Profile pointer; A-8 Requirements Specification Package; A-9 Feature Inventory Document; A-10 NFR pointer |
| Architecture and design detail | A-11 Data Model / ERD Document; A-12 API and Integration Contract |
| Development preparation | A-13 Module and File Planning Document; A-14 Environment and Delivery Strategy; A-15 Development Plan |
| Testing and validation | A-16 Test Strategy; A-17 Acceptance Test Scenarios; A-18 QA Results; A-19 Bug Report; A-20 Validation Sign-Off |
| Release preparation | A-21 Release Readiness Checklist; A-22 Known Issues List; A-23 Release Notes; A-24 Rollback Plan; A-25 Security Review Results; A-25.1 Security Review Checklist; A-26 Final Approval Record |
| Deployment | A-27 Deployment Checklist; A-28 Deployment Record; A-29 Migration Record; A-30 Environment Configuration Record; A-31 Rollback Confirmation |
| Maintenance and improvement | A-32 Maintenance Plan; A-33 Incident / Production Issue Record; A-34 Maintenance Change Request; A-35 Maintenance Backlog; A-36 Operational Metrics / Actuals Review; A-37 Maintenance Release / Patch Record |
| Post-release review | A-38 Post-Release Review; A-39 Lessons Learned Record; A-40 Improvement Recommendations; A-41 Process Improvement Notes |

## Template Details

The following templates consolidate controlled lifecycle evidence. Use them when a project needs consistent structure, approval records, section numbering, traceability hooks, and gate evidence compatible with the Master Lifecycle and USSM.

### Template Metadata Convention

Every completed lifecycle artifact should include stable metadata near the top of the document unless the template provides a stricter header:

```text
Artifact ID:
Template ID:
Artifact Title:
Related Product ID (PRD-XXX), if any:
Related PCL Code, if any:
Lifecycle Phase:
Related Gate(s):
Owner:
Prepared By:
Version / Revision:
Date:
Status:
```

Apply `STD-ENG-001` as follows:

- Use lifecycle-local artifact IDs for ordinary lifecycle evidence unless a CYBERCUBE governance registry assigns a Namespace G ID.
- Use `PRD-XXX`, `MOD-XXX`, or future Namespace G IDs only when assigned by the authoritative registry.
- Use CC-PID for public entity identifiers where APIs, URLs, logs, support workflows, or UI expose entity references; do not expose raw database primary keys externally.
- Follow Namespace M conventions for module, component, directory, export, and file names in implementation-facing templates.
- If an existing artifact cannot use this metadata block, record an equivalent mapping in the traceability matrix.

### Template A-0 — Idea Capture Form

**Purpose:** Record a software idea before it becomes an approved project. The form captures the early idea, the problem it may solve, target users, expected value, current situation, high-level solution, risks, constraints, complexity, recommendation, and approval status. The form does **not** approve the project.

**Lifecycle:** Produced in **Phase 1 — Idea Capture** and reviewed at **G1 — Idea Accepted for Analysis** before moving to **Phase 2 — Problem Definition**.

**Primary owner:** Idea Sponsor (the person who proposes the idea).

**Supporting reviewers:**

| Role | Responsibility |
| --- | --- |
| Project Owner | Reviews fit with business goals. |
| Technical Lead | Provides early technical feasibility comments. |
| Product / Business Reviewer | Evaluates potential customer or business value. |
| Standards / Governance Reviewer | Confirms the idea should continue into the formal lifecycle. |

#### Required sections

1. Idea Title
2. Idea Sponsor and Date Submitted
3. Short Idea Summary
4. Problem or Opportunity
5. Target Users or Beneficiaries
6. Current Situation
7. Expected Benefit
8. Possible Software Solution
9. Urgency or Timing
10. Known Constraints
11. Known Risks or Concerns
12. Similar Existing Solutions
13. Initial Complexity Estimate
14. Initial PRCS Context
15. Recommendation
16. Approval Status

#### Form body

##### 1. Idea Title — Required

Provide a short, clear name for the idea.

```text
Idea Title:
```

##### 2. Idea Sponsor and Date Submitted — Required

Identify who proposed the idea and when.

```text
Submitted By:
Role:
Contact:
Date Submitted:
```

##### 3. Short Idea Summary — Required

Describe the idea in 2–4 sentences.

```text
Summary:
```

##### 4. Problem or Opportunity — Required

Describe the problem, pain point, inefficiency, opportunity, or need that inspired the idea.

```text
Problem / Opportunity:
```

Helpful questions: What is happening? Why does it matter? Who is affected? What gets worse if nothing changes?

##### 5. Target Users or Beneficiaries — Required

Identify who would use or benefit from the software.

```text
Primary Users:
Secondary Users:
Business Beneficiaries:
```

Examples: small business owners, internal employees, customers, administrators, managers, nonprofit staff, field workers, students, developers.

##### 6. Current Situation — Required

Explain how the problem is handled today.

```text
Current Process / Current Solution:
```

Helpful questions: Is the work manual? Spreadsheet-based? Email-based? Handled by another system? What is frustrating or inefficient?

##### 7. Expected Benefit — Required

Describe the value this idea could create.

```text
Expected Benefit:
```

Possible benefits: saves time, reduces errors, improves customer experience, increases revenue, reduces cost, improves reporting, automates repetitive work, improves compliance, improves visibility, improves decision-making.

##### 8. Possible Software Solution — Required

Describe the possible software solution at a high level. Stay simple — do **not** design the full system yet.

```text
Possible Solution:
```

Example: *A small web portal where customers can submit service requests and track their status.*

##### 9. Urgency or Timing — Required

```text
Urgency:        (Low | Medium | High | Critical)
Timing Notes:
```

##### 10. Known Constraints — Required if known

List any limitations that may affect the project.

```text
Known Constraints:
```

Examples: limited budget, limited development time, must integrate with existing system, must support mobile users, must follow security requirements, must be ready before a specific date, requires customer approval, requires third-party API.

##### 11. Known Risks or Concerns — Required if known

```text
Known Risks / Concerns:
```

Examples: unclear user need, unknown technical complexity, possible privacy concerns, integration may be difficult, high maintenance burden, no confirmed customer demand.

##### 12. Similar Existing Solutions — Optional but recommended

List any existing tools, competitors, internal systems, or manual processes that already solve part of the problem.

```text
Similar Existing Solutions:
```

Helpful questions: Is there already software for this? Why is the existing option insufficient? Could we buy instead of build? Could we improve an existing tool?

##### 13. Initial Complexity Estimate — Required

Provide an early rough estimate. See `23. Project Complexity Levels.md` for the canonical rubric.

```text
Initial Complexity:    (Small | Medium | Large | Unknown)
```

##### 14. Initial PRCS Context — Required if known

Record early product-registry context without requiring final classification at G1.

```text
Existing Product ID (PRD-XXX), if any:
Candidate New Product? Yes / No / Unknown:
Candidate Product / Service Name:
Tentative PCL Code (PCL-L.D.E.C), if known:
Likely Domain Tags:
Function Descriptor Notes:
Work Type Tag(s), if pre-product or cross-cutting:
PRCS Notes / Unknowns:
```

##### 15. Recommendation — Required

State what should happen next.

```text
Recommendation:    (Reject | Defer | Research More | Move to Problem Definition)
```

##### 16. Approval Status — Required

Record the current status of the idea.

```text
Approval Status:
Reviewer:
Review Date:
Decision Notes:
```

| Status | Meaning |
| --- | --- |
| Draft | The idea is still being written. |
| Submitted | The idea has been formally submitted for review. |
| Under Review | A reviewer is evaluating whether it should continue. |
| Accepted for Problem Definition | The idea may move to Phase 2. |
| Research More | The idea needs additional input before G1 can be decided. |
| Deferred | The idea is valid but will not continue now. |
| Rejected | The idea will not continue. |

#### Completion criteria

The Idea Capture Form is complete when every required section above has a value, the recommendation is recorded, and an approval status has been assigned by a named reviewer with a review date. Vague, unscoped, or unowned ideas must be returned to the sponsor before the form is accepted.

---

### Template A-0.1 — Problem Definition Document

**Purpose:** Define the real problem behind a software idea before the project is evaluated, selected, planned, or designed. The document ensures work rests on a **real need**, not only an interesting feature, assumption, trend, or technology idea.

**Lifecycle:** Produced in **Phase 2 — Problem Definition** and reviewed at **G2 — Problem Validated** before moving to **Phase 3 — Project Evaluation and Selection**. Approval requires at least one independent validation source.

**Primary owner:** Project Owner or Idea Sponsor.

**Supporting owners:**

| Role | Responsibility |
| --- | --- |
| Idea Sponsor | Explains the original idea and motivation. |
| Project Owner | Owns the problem definition and review process. |
| Business reviewer | Confirms business relevance and expected value. |
| User / customer representative | Confirms whether the problem is real. |
| Technical lead | Identifies early technical concerns if needed. |
| Governance reviewer | Confirms the document is complete enough to continue. |

For a solo project, the same person may perform several roles.

#### Required sections

1. Document Metadata
2. Problem Title
3. Problem Summary
4. Background and Context
5. Affected Users or Stakeholders
6. Current Process or Current Solution
7. Pain Points and Limitations
8. Problem Frequency
9. Problem Severity
10. Evidence or Observations
11. Business Impact
12. User Impact
13. Operational Impact
14. Why the Current Solution Is Insufficient
15. Desired Outcome
16. Initial Success Indicators
17. Assumptions
18. Constraints
19. Risks of Not Solving the Problem
20. Out of Scope
21. Recommendation
22. Approval Status

#### Form body

##### 1. Document Metadata

```text
Document Title:
Project / Idea Name:
Document Version:
Status:
Owner:
Prepared By:
Date Created:
Last Updated:
Related Idea Capture Form:
```

Suggested statuses: Draft · Submitted · Under Review · Validated — Proceed to Selection · Return to Phase 1 · Deferred · Rejected

##### 2. Problem Title

Provide a short, clear title for the problem.

```text
Problem Title:
```

Example: *Small contractors lose billable equipment usage because telematics data is not connected to invoicing.*

##### 3. Problem Summary

Describe the problem in one short paragraph (who, what is wrong, why it matters, consequence).

```text
Problem Summary:
```

Example: *Small equipment rental and wet-hire companies often track machine usage manually or across disconnected systems. Because operating hours, idle time, attachment usage, and fuel data are not automatically connected to invoices, billable usage may be missed, disputed, or delayed, creating revenue loss, administrative burden, and weaker billing accuracy.*

##### 4. Background and Context

Explain the situation that created the problem.

```text
Background / Context:
```

Helpful questions: Where does it happen? When? Who is involved? What workflow creates the issue? What tools or manual steps are used?

##### 5. Affected Users or Stakeholders

```text
Primary Users Affected:
Secondary Users Affected:
Business Stakeholders:
External Stakeholders:
```

Examples: customers, employees, managers, administrators, field workers, accounting staff, support teams, compliance reviewers.

##### 6. Current Process or Current Solution

```text
Current Process / Current Solution:
```

Helpful questions: Manual? Spreadsheets? Email? Phone? Existing software? Depends on one person's memory? Data copied between systems?

##### 7. Pain Points and Limitations

```text
Pain Points:
```

Examples: manual data entry; duplicated work; no real-time status; errors discovered late; slow reports; customers must call for updates; records hard to find; spreadsheet dependence; weak audit trail; lost time, money, or visibility.

##### 8. Problem Frequency

```text
Problem Frequency:
```

Suggested values: Rare · Occasional · Frequent · Daily · Continuous · Unknown

```text
Frequency Explanation:
```

Example: *Weekly during invoice preparation; daily when field usage data is manually reviewed.*

##### 9. Problem Severity

```text
Problem Severity:
```

Suggested values: Low · Medium · High · Critical · Unknown

| Severity | Meaning |
| --- | --- |
| Low | Annoying but little operational effect. |
| Medium | Delays, confusion, moderate inefficiency. |
| High | Revenue loss, customer frustration, compliance risk, or major burden. |
| Critical | Blocks operations, serious legal/security risk, or major financial damage. |

```text
Severity Explanation:
```

##### 10. Evidence or Observations

```text
Evidence / Observations:
Validation Source(s):
```

Evidence may include: user complaints; interviews; support tickets; screenshots; estimates; time tracking; errors; analytics; incidents; research; observation.

If no independent validation source exists yet:

```text
Evidence Status: Not yet validated
Additional Research Needed:
```

##### 11. Business Impact

```text
Business Impact:
```

Possible impacts: revenue loss; labor cost; delays; poor CX; lost productivity; missed opportunities; compliance exposure; bad reporting; slow decisions; higher support load.

##### 12. User Impact

```text
User Impact:
```

Does it waste time? Cause frustration? Force rework? Create confusion? Drive support dependence? Block tasks?

##### 13. Operational Impact

```text
Operational Impact:
```

Examples: more manual work; delayed approvals; poor visibility; fragmented records; inconsistent processes; harder maintenance.

##### 14. Why the Current Solution Is Insufficient

```text
Why Current Solution Is Insufficient:
```

What fails today? Why not scale? Unreliable? Too slow? Error-prone? Too costly? Frustrating?

##### 15. Desired Outcome

Describe the **result**, not the detailed software solution.

```text
Desired Outcome:
```

Good: *The business calculates accurate billable equipment usage with less manual work and fewer invoice disputes.*
Weak: *Build a dashboard with five tabs and a blue button.*

##### 16. Initial Success Indicators

```text
Initial Success Indicators:
```

Examples: reduced manual entry; shorter invoice prep time; fewer disputes; fewer support requests; automated reports; adoption targets met.

##### 17. Assumptions

```text
Assumptions:
```

Examples: users will adopt a portal; required data is accessible; integration API exists; manual process causes measurable delay.

##### 18. Constraints

```text
Constraints:
```

Examples: budget; timeline; team size; required integrations; mobile need; security rules; roles; data sensitivity.

##### 19. Risks of Not Solving the Problem

```text
Risks of Not Solving:
```

Examples: continued revenue loss; persistent manual work; frustration; errors; support burden; missed market opportunity; compliance exposure; lost competitiveness.

##### 20. Out of Scope

```text
Out of Scope:
```

Examples: final architecture; technology stack; detailed UI; schema; implementation plan; pricing; timeline; full feature list, so this phase does not become design.

##### 21. Recommendation

```text
Recommendation:
```

Suggested values: Validated — Proceed to Selection · Return to Phase 1 · Defer · Reject

```text
Recommendation Rationale:
```

##### 22. Approval Status

```text
Approval Status:
Reviewer:
Review Date:
Decision Notes:
```

| Status | Meaning |
| --- | --- |
| Draft | Still being written. |
| Submitted | Submitted for review. |
| Under Review | Reviewer evaluating. |
| Validated — Proceed to Selection | Clear enough and independently validated to proceed to Phase 3. |
| Return to Phase 1 | The idea/problem framing is wrong or insufficient and should return to intake. |
| Deferred | Valid but not continuing now. |
| Rejected | Will not continue. |

#### Completion criteria

The Problem Definition Document is complete when:

1. The problem has a clear title and plain-language summary.
2. Background and context are documented.
3. Affected users and stakeholders are identified.
4. The current process or solution is described.
5. Pain points, frequency, and severity are recorded.
6. At least one independent validation source is documented for G2 approval, or the document is explicitly not ready for G2 approval.
7. Business, user, and operational impacts are explained.
8. Insufficiency of the current approach is explained; desired outcome is stated.
9. Initial success indicators are defined; assumptions and constraints listed.
10. Risks of not solving and out-of-scope items are documented.
11. A recommendation is recorded and approval status assigned by a named reviewer with date.

---

### Template A-1 — Customer Requirement Specification (CRS)

**Purpose:** Capture stakeholder-oriented needs, boundaries, and constraints before detailed engineering requirements.

#### 1. Introduction

| Section | Content |
| --- | --- |
| 1.1 Purpose | Why the CRS exists; its role in the lifecycle. |
| 1.2 Scope | System boundaries; included features; external systems/interfaces. |
| 1.3 Definitions, acronyms, abbreviations | Glossary for terms used in the document. |

#### 2. Overall description

| Section | Content |
| --- | --- |
| 2.1 Product perspective | Product context; market position; target users and needs. |
| 2.2 Product functions | Primary functions; inputs, outputs, processing steps per function. |
| 2.3 User characteristics | Users; technical level; roles and permissions. |
| 2.4 General constraints | Limitations (performance, security, hardware); regulatory/compliance. |

#### 3. Specific requirements

| Section | Content |
| --- | --- |
| 3.1 Functional requirements | Functions the product must perform; I/O and processing; performance where stated as stakeholder-facing. |
| 3.2 Non-functional requirements | Usability, reliability, performance, security, maintainability; quality attributes (accuracy, completeness, consistency). |
| 3.3 Design constraints | Limits on technology, platform, or architecture (if known at this stage). |

#### 4. External interface requirements

| Section | Content |
| --- | --- |
| 4.1 User interfaces | UI expectations; layout/navigation; usability standards. |
| 4.2 Hardware interfaces | Devices, protocols, drivers. |
| 4.3 Software interfaces | Components, protocols, data formats. |

#### 5. Standards and design constraints

| Section | Content |
| --- | --- |
| 5.1 Standards compliance | Applicable standards or regulations. |
| 5.2 Design constraints | Technology/platform/architecture limits. |

#### 6. Appendices

| Section | Content |
| --- | --- |
| 6.1 Use case diagrams | Diagrams and use case descriptions. |
| 6.2 Data dictionary | Data elements, structures, flows. |
| 6.3 User interface mockups | Prototypes or mockups. |
| 6.4 References | External references. |

**Tips:** Clear language; consistent formatting; stakeholder review; version control; revisit as the project evolves.

---

### Template A-2 — Software Requirement Specification (SRS)

**Purpose:** Translate CRS into **testable**, engineering-ready requirements (objectives, detailed requirements, assumptions, risks).

**Typical outline**

1. **Introduction** — Purpose; intended audience; intended use; product scope; definitions and acronyms.
2. **Overall description** — User needs; assumptions and dependencies.
3. **System features and requirements** — Functional requirements; external interface requirements; system features; non-functional requirements.

**Collaborative SRS shaping:** Workshop and refine **project objectives**, detailed **requirements**, **anticipated challenges**, **opportunities**, and **risks** so the SRS doubles as the aligned engineering vision—not only a flat requirement list.

**Alternate section numbering (IEEE-style introduction)**

Some teams prefer explicit numbering compatible with classic SRS templates:

1. Introduction — 1.1 Purpose · 1.2 Intended audience · 1.3 Intended use · 1.4 Product scope · 1.5 Definitions and acronyms  
2. Overall description — 2.1 User needs · 2.2 Assumptions and dependencies  
3. System features and requirements — 3.1 Functional · 3.2 External interface · 3.3 System features · 3.4 Non-functional requirements  

Either numbering style is acceptable if applied consistently and trace IDs remain stable.

#### Introduction — what to establish

- **Intended audience and use:** Who reads the SRS (engineering, QA, PM, stakeholders) and how they use it.
- **Product scope:** Benefits, objectives, goals tied to business outcomes.
- **Definitions:** Terms and acronyms; link to a glossary when terminology is heavy.

#### Overall description — product narrative

- Why the product is needed; who it is for; new vs. add-on vs. integration with other products.
- **User needs:** Primary vs. secondary users; organizational roles; purchaser vs. end user; domain-specific users (e.g. patient in regulated domains).
- **Assumptions and dependencies:** Technical assumptions (platform, stack); dependencies on other systems or reused components.

#### Detailed requirements — categories

- **Functional requirements:** Capabilities the product must provide; domain-specific subsets where applicable; interaction with other tools.
- **External interface requirements:** How the product connects to users, hardware, software, communications (especially embedded systems).
- **System features:** Required capabilities for the system to function as specified.
- **Non-functional requirements:** Performance, safety, security, usability, scalability; sector-specific regulations may mandate explicit safety/traceability.

Use IEEE or organizational guidance for SRS authoring where membership or standards apply.

---

### Template A-3 — Design Document Specification (DDS / SDD)

**Purpose:** Describe architecture, modules, interfaces, data, and verification approach so implementation (coding) can proceed consistently and SRS/DDS traceability can be maintained.

**Lifecycle:** During development, engineers implement against the DDS and verify alignment with the SRS (classic SDLC **Design** informs **Construction**).

#### 1. Introduction

| Section | Content |
| --- | --- |
| 1.1 Purpose | Role of the SDD/DDS in the lifecycle. |
| 1.2 Scope | Design boundaries; features; external systems/interfaces. |
| 1.3 Definitions, acronyms, abbreviations | Glossary. |

#### 2. Overall design

| Section | Content |
| --- | --- |
| 2.1 Architectural design | Architecture; major components and interactions; patterns/frameworks. |
| 2.2 User interface design | UI elements, layout, navigation; UX principles. |
| 2.3 Data design | Data model; entities and relationships; storage (databases, files). |

#### 3. Detailed design

| Section | Content |
| --- | --- |
| 3.1 Module design | Per-module functionality, inputs, outputs, interfaces; algorithms and structures. |
| 3.2 Algorithm design | Algorithms; I/O; complexity (time/space) where useful. |
| 3.3 Interface design | Module and external interfaces; formats, protocols, error handling. |

#### 4. Design constraints and assumptions

| Section | Content |
| --- | --- |
| 4.1 Constraints | Performance, security, hardware, etc. |
| 4.2 Assumptions | Assumptions and impact if false. |

#### 5. Design verification and validation

| Section | Content |
| --- | --- |
| 5.1 Verification | Reviews, inspections, walkthroughs that design meets requirements. |
| 5.2 Validation | Simulations, prototypes, testing that the design is correct for intent. |

#### 6. Appendices

| Section | Content |
| --- | --- |
| 6.1 Detailed design diagrams | UML, flowcharts, etc. |
| 6.2 Code examples | Snippets illustrating design decisions. |
| 6.3 References | Citations. |

**Tips:** Clear language; update as design evolves; stakeholder review; version control.

---

### Template A-3.1 — Project Selection Scorecard

**Purpose:** Compare candidate initiatives consistently during **Phase 3 — Project Evaluation and Selection** and produce the selection evidence that feeds **Phase 4 — Feasibility and Business Case**. This scorecard is a **selection signal**; full **G3 — Project Selected** evidence is completed after Phase 4 adds feasibility and business-case evidence.

**Required inputs:**

- Idea Capture Form (Template A-0).
- Problem Definition Document (Template A-0.1) with **G2 — Validated — Proceed to Selection** outcome.
- Business Field Report (Template A-4) or approved waiver.
- Strategic priorities, portfolio constraints, or sponsor goals.

#### 1. Metadata

```text
Scorecard Name:
Candidate / Initiative:
Owner:
Prepared By:
Review Date:
Decision Body / Reviewer:
Related Problem Definition:
Related Business Field Report:
Existing Product ID (PRD-XXX), if any:
Candidate New Product? Yes / No / Unknown:
Candidate PCL Code (PCL-L.D.E.C), if known:
Likely Domain Tags:
Work Type Tag(s), if pre-product or cross-cutting:
```

#### 2. Selection Criteria

Define scoring criteria before scoring candidates. Use a 1–5 scale unless the program defines another method.

| Criterion | Weight | Score (1–5) | Weighted Score | Evidence / Rationale |
| --- | ---: | ---: | ---: | --- |
| Strategic alignment |  |  |  |  |
| Customer / user value |  |  |  |  |
| Business value |  |  |  |  |
| Market / opportunity strength |  |  |  |  |
| Feasibility signal |  |  |  |  |
| Risk / uncertainty |  |  |  |  |
| Cost / capacity fit |  |  |  |  |
| Compliance / security exposure |  |  |  |  |

#### 3. Candidate Comparison

Use when multiple initiatives or solution directions are being compared.

| Candidate | Total Score | Rank | Recommendation | Key Reason |
| --- | ---: | ---: | --- | --- |
|  |  |  | Proceed to feasibility / Pivot / Defer / Stop |  |

#### 4. Recommendation

```text

```

#### Completion criteria

The scorecard is complete when criteria and weights are defined, candidate scores are recorded, evidence links support the scoring, the recommendation is stated, assumptions/dependencies are listed, and reviewer/date are recorded. It is not a substitute for Phase 4 feasibility and business-case approval.
